## \#630 Posted by: cmatson Posted at: 2016-07-05T05:25:21.022Z Reads: 199

```
[quote="barajabali, post:627, topic:1002"]
Genera question- are hub motors generally more efficient in terms of range compared to satellite set ups?
[/quote]

I think if you just cruise the flats, range won't be that different. if you are constantly stopping, accelerating, and going up hills, a satellite will for sure win. 

The hubs just draw more amps than a satellite during acceleration and hill climbing, but draw less during normal cruising.
```

```
Mechanically efficient and electrically inefficient. Maybe
```

## \#662 Posted by: Mobutusan Posted at: 2016-07-06T10:30:16.598Z Reads: 209

```
@Hummie & @devin
What do you think about this idea? Seems easy enough to 3d print for someone who knows what they're doing. 
Basically, it's a somewhat thin plastic or metal, donut shaped plate that sits outboard of the motor on spacers, to allow airflow between the plate and motor. Cross section shape is a vertically oriented airfoil type shape that's flat on the outside face and convex on the inside face. Theoretically, the air flowing over the curved, convex side should result in a lower pressure area relative to the flat side, and I would also assume, relative to the motor, in the same way an airfoil creates lift. The higher relative pressure and heat inside the motor would be drawn out of the motor to balance the low pressure areas between the inside face of the plate and the motor vent holes, while the air on the outside flows smoothly over the flat side of the plate. I even drew a pretty picture to illustrate my madness.

You guys feeling this concept at all, or am I completely off my nut? :smile:

<img src="/uploads/db1493/original/2X/7/75150ffce3c7b8fbaae42a896c55e77e9bc03818.jpg" width="690" height="388">
```

## \#681 Posted by: HTownBomber Posted at: 2016-07-06T16:31:27.455Z Reads: 186

```
Maybe. You're not capturing inefficiencies such as cogging, hysteresis, current leakage, etc and Hummie's motors have some issues there when under load. The motor heat is a byproduct of inefficiency. 

Most bldc motors are ~70-80% efficient but his might be on the low side of that.
```

---

## \#684 Posted by: Hummie Posted at: 2016-07-06T17:38:35.869Z Reads: 203

```
Hi everyone. Pylon flyer!  He's the rubber man and helped a lot getting these made.  If we should figure out what to change..a core for the softer 80duro rubber or some filler towards the hub to hold shape.  I find the 80 will deteriorate and get soft and lose they're tight fit after a while while the 90 is solid but I like the feel of the 80 

 The wheels are 80x56 and if they aren't exactly that then kegels aren't exactly that either.  I'm pretty sure they're that but Shawn used a measuring tool and hard to argue with that. 

 Everyone is into the 3D print part!  Today I should have one done that seems a sure design.  Pretty much copying what's been done be big businesses with lots of research...can't go wrong. 
The versions you guys did..I can send u the 3D file for the flange it should replace and you can make sure it will fit and give it a shot but as I say I've been down the wrong roads a couple times and it's not so intuitive what happens and the best way is to simply spend the bucks (wonder how much that place will cost) and give it a shot.  Anorak from what I'm seeing lately with the centrifugal force and the air going out you want the hole that the air that the air enters and exits through the piece to be the same sq diameter.  Not sure how important it is, and with your design might just convert the strong force to volume as it exits a bigger hole and it's hard to find info
Sean I have no idea how that would work and I if u think it will work I hope you print it and give it a shot.

These are smaller motors than Jacobs.  Depending on where u ride and how u ride maybe get Jacobs if u want more torque.  When I made these I wanted all within the kegel shape and that's what they are.  

These motors being smaller could make more heat, or there smaller size could make it harder to absorb the heat.  Honestly though if u ride them and see you'll find it all depends on how many amps u want to put into them.  Set it to a 30amp limit or something like that and u don't have to worry.  Set it to 70 amps to each motor and then you have an evolve gt eater that just needs to rest after so many blasts up hills
```

## \#708 Posted by: Justin Posted at: 2016-07-07T14:46:37.311Z Reads: 190

```
I just got back from doing some simple temperature checks, tried out some hills and went as fast as i could for a long continuous run (scary as hell, never even went full throttle). The temperatures never got above 70ºc / 160ºF, when riding normal the temp seems to be hanging around 50ºc / 122ºF. Didn't encounter any problems going up hills, could even keep accelerating half-way going up.

The urethane also reached 50ºc and sometimes started making weird ticking sounds, so i stopped to see what the cause was and didn't hear the sounds running the wheels without any load. Eventually i found out that i was caused by small pebbles getting stuck in the urethane. The Orangatang Kegels i'm running in the front don't seem to have this problem. I think @Hummie send me his first batch of fast curing urethane stuff, so i think the newer wheels with different type of urethane don't have this problem anymore.

Btw. i also did some unpowered boarding with these wheels for the first time today, and they roll buttery smooth!
```

---
## \#709 Posted by: whitepony Posted at: 2016-07-07T14:52:56.530Z Reads: 188

```
maybe thats just the effect of warm urethane. when I still raced motorbikes, the tyres would be around 80-90°C and become supersoft and supersticky, gobbling up ANYTHING they'd roll over. :smiley:

thats why I said: maybe its pretty cool to have your own urethane heater. when I carve hard, my flywheels become really warm, measured up to 50°C. and once they are warm, they noticeably grip better and you can push even harder! :heart:
```

## \#714 Posted by: Nordle Posted at: 2016-07-07T17:34:28.155Z Reads: 184

```
@Hummie What's the lowest kv you ever tried on these?
If i calculate max speed for your 90kv hubs:
80mm dia....results in 0,00025132km per revolution [or circumference]
44V [12S] * 90kv = 3960rp/m             *60= 237.600rp/h
circ * rp/h = 59,7km/h = 37,1mp/h
that's a bit fast i think, i have a 85kv hubmotor (80mm wheels) and i would go lower if i could but diy options sometimes are a bit limited.. Just think a motor runs on its max efficency on about 90%of his rpm, still 53km/h, that's not the average speed i ride. Thats why i think our motors get hot and consume much.
Also did you ever try hall sensors? I built some into my new motors but only testet them on the bench, just from the load i gave them with my hands i imagine startup from zero and braking will be much better..
```

## \#727 Posted by: lowGuido Posted at: 2016-07-07T23:31:03.685Z Reads: 190

```
Ok,  this is going to be the last thing I post in this thread because I am just sick of all the if this, maybe that.

I am just going to post the exact experience that I had with the motors sent to Me.
Facts:
flat street, (maybe an ever so slight down hill) the same for both tests.
batteries charged to the same voltage for both tests.
same board used for both tests
both tests conducted by pushing full throttle and holding it full for the length of the street
speed measured by GPS app Strava for both tests
same VESC used but motor parameters changed to be appropriate for each motor (this is the only difference in the whole testing procedure)
assumed kv for hummis 80kv
assumed kv for jacobs 70kv

jacobs motors were faster, not by just a little, but at least 10% faster

that's my experience. and I'm not going to bother entertaining anyone else's **excuses** by posting in this thread again.
LowGuido OUT. (mic drop)
```

## \#730 Posted by: Jack Posted at: 2016-07-08T02:57:00.140Z Reads: 187

```
@Titoxd10001 Unless you have a hex or square shaped shaft on the truck and hub motor to accept it it you won't have a easily removable motor. Your diagram won't work, if you glue the motor onto that extension then the torque of acceleration and breaking will just loosen the extension piece, same as if you just screwed the motor on.

Oh and the hex shaft idea would be very expensive. Not really the shaft more the cost of broaching a hex into the hub motor. that is $$$
```

---
## \#731 Posted by: Titoxd10001 Posted at: 2016-07-08T03:21:14.694Z Reads: 184

```
The adapter would be glued on the truck or even welded instead of the motor. The hex keeps the motor from spinning. Just an idea 

Hubs are expensive what is like $400+ for steel. I most worries about the bearings because there is always puddles and as it is now you can't replace them
```

---
## \#732 Posted by: Wanderer Posted at: 2016-07-08T04:05:38.054Z Reads: 189

```
It is a good idea. Maybe not perfectly ideal where you can always use the truck like a normal truck because you would have to weld/glue on, but 99% of the time people wont care about being able to use the truck normally. Keeps the unit servicable, which is nice and should be where all designs of hubmotors go in the future.

Had an idea somewhat like this except was thinking splines, but a hex would be far easier to machine. Also if you did want to go the route of making the truck usable in normal longboarding situations, you could put in a couple hefty grub screws that would sit just a bit lower than the outside serface of the hex so you can still slide the motors overtop. A screwed on hex extension with two large grub screws that you can really crank down on, would be good and solid.
```

## \#752 Posted by: Hummie Posted at: 2016-07-09T06:23:53.077Z Reads: 187

```
I get it now.  Good idea. As it is now, as you know, the big bearing with the hubmotors is trapped unless the motor's glue is cracked loose. It's a big bearing and should last a long time but still it needs to come out.  Ive tested red loctite and it will hold the torque of the motor but is still easy enough to spin off with a two pin spanner wrench that fits in the stator holes and with all steel thats good enough and simpler.   It works. It's easy and cheap.  To have another piece made as you drew I think it would be good, maybe preferable, but I don't want to add the complexity and cost.  Long two pin spanners that fit the holes aren't hard to come by.
```

## \#755 Posted by: baxter Posted at: 2016-07-09T10:27:18.258Z Reads: 192

```
Just a thought: 

Have you considered designing two hub caps to create a pressure differential from one side of the hub to the other? 

Based on the designs I have seen, and my understanding of the Bernoulli principle, it  would mean that two hub caps could be designed to work in symmetry. One to increase air pressure - forcing colder air into the hub, and the other on the other side of the hub to decrease air pressure within the hub (pulling warmer air from the hub).
   
@Lizardking0069's photo of a Yuneec motor showed a single impeller on the motor shaft, why not have one on each side of the hub motor?  

I am sure someone could come up with a 3D printed fan design to go on both sides of the hub to increase the efficiency and volume of airflow through the vent holes.

What do you guys think?
```

## \#758 Posted by: Justin Posted at: 2016-07-09T21:16:23.693Z Reads: 201

```
So today i finally had enough confidence to go full throttle and did a pretty fast run for 20+ minutes, the outside weather temp was +/- 24ºc and the motors measured up to ~100ºC after the run. The cool thing is that the truck actually works as a heatsink (was to hot to mallgrab the board, lol). But i also want to emphasize that using **heat resistant bonding / epoxy is very important!** (for the guys who mount the wheels them selfs). Top speed maxed out at 41.8km/h using a 10S s.p.a.c.e-cell. Don't think ill ever ride this fast on normal commutes though, but it sure is fun as hell to over take people on racing bikes and scooters haha. Confidence is growing and love my board more and more every ride. Going to order a full face helmet now though, just to be safe :)

<img src="/uploads/db1493/original/2X/2/2b8a95176cfdbc228d390a42c6c2e559081efb28.jpg" width="690" height="138">

<img src="/uploads/db1493/original/2X/7/79846d44b169e4eff8b42ef0fad6488cf11ddf45.jpg" width="690" height="138">
```

## \#760 Posted by: torqueboards Posted at: 2016-07-10T01:52:25.984Z Reads: 192

```
I think you just need to give it more throttle then a normal belt drive setup. From riding my own, I noticed they don't like anything below 5amps of power but once you give it that initial start-up power then you should be fine. I'd imagine it's the same with the hub motors you have but not sure haven't tested SteelHubs yet.

From Jacob's youtube videos, he set his Start Up Boost to 0.090 for his hub motors.
```

## \#762 Posted by: Hummie Posted at: 2016-07-10T07:58:01.592Z Reads: 209

```
 good news!. Tomorrow I'll borrow a camera and I think I'll be able to show you some awesome mountain climbing.      I finally got around to testin the temps of the new steel motors I've been riding. Stunningly cool.  I started with thermometer in hand riding with evohyax with him on the blue goo version and we went up the local hill to see what temps we'd hit.  To see how bad his were.   There are different parts on the motor we took measurements but overall it was something like 175 to 115. He has blue goo but the blue goo were not much hotter than the regular purple.  later after he left I did laps on the same bump at full speed (same one I do in the videos) and hit max 140f before my battery died.  Why is the steel cooler? I don't know They're almost the same motor other than the center is steel, the magnets are less strong but with a smaller airgap.  On a magnet pull calculator they show almost the same though. Less strong closer vs stronger farther away.   My first set of motors that I never successfully rewound after they shorted against the housing also had the same small airgap and I remember them being much cooler. ( I asked them to add a smidgen more airgap for mechanical safety's sake on the big purple batch) .   I have no answers only my trusty thermometer's results and guesses so far today but tomorrow..who cares and I want to go up a mountain!!  And then down!!
```

---
## \#763 Posted by: appelton Posted at: 2016-07-10T09:13:37.341Z Reads: 213

```
Today I was looking at Stary's hub motor...and with each version they 've moved the motor more to the back, outside the urethane. They started with something like this: 

http://www.znjchina.com/d/file/2015-06-21/11f10efd94a0b5e739971c57013bcbb0.jpg

to finally end up with something like this

http://imgur.com/nArQob9

They didn't go all the way like CARVON, but enough to make it look good and get rid of the heat. 

So maybe you could include some of these changes in the next version:

- bigger motor = less heat
- motor moved a little bit outside the urethane = less heat
- proper hubcap that covers motor outside the urethane so it can act as a heatsink delivering the heat to the truck axel
- if the motor is moved outside maybe there will be enough place for putting this fan inside
- bigger urethane 90mm for higher top speed as well as for comfortable riding. When it comes to urethane nobody mentions it here. But, the truth is once you go 90mm there is no going back 83mm :slight_smile:
```

## \#767 Posted by: Lizardking0069 Posted at: 2016-07-10T17:53:50.987Z Reads: 203

```
Well the smaller the air gap means better saturation which means better efficiency and less heat. In RC cars, a correctly geared motor will heat up to a desired temp of 150F. So being around 140 is perfect.
```

---
## \#768 Posted by: Hummie Posted at: 2016-07-10T18:12:06.637Z Reads: 209

```
Why would u want to be 150f?  Heat Increases electrical resistance, slightly decreases magnet strength and slowly will deteriorate winding enamel. I've heard of heating batteries for better performance. 
Why .1mm airgap change would have huge consequences I don't know and I almost don't believe my thermometer numbers.  I always equated a motor magnet's pull force (as shown on j and b magnets calculator) as the revealation of its possible performance.   I'm stunned. Happy.  won't put the strain on things the high heat was doing, can go back to 80 duro rubber and not worry about it baking.  No more forced air cooling attempts for me
```

---
## \#769 Posted by: Lizardking0069 Posted at: 2016-07-10T18:55:32.899Z Reads: 198

```
I guess i used the wring word there. Its not a desired temp, but the normal temp for good gearing.
```

## \#776 Posted by: Hummie Posted at: 2016-07-11T00:25:58.028Z Reads: 211

```
Haha. Everyone videos are of their shoes. One day I'll finally make a cool first person video with good  sound
Hope ur riding on FOC. For me even with the vesc on 12s and the bldc program it's not nearly as great. 

These are delta wound. But if u were to change them to wye I think it would be hard as the motor wires are converted to a single strand. And there's glue. I find reterminating too frustrating.  If it was motor strands that came all the way out maybe go through strand by strand and that'd be easy. Going in the motor to take apart the glued connections between windings is ridiculously hard I find. 

Damn j wanted to test these up the mountains in the north bay and am on route there now but riding earlier this morning I hit the second low voltage cut off and rode a bit more going it's max speed of maybe 5mph. I had the limit set to 3.5 a cell. I rode around for not even long and also did a downhill( thinking id be charging) get home and every one of my cells is sucked to 2.7 or less and one at .6!  Did riding downhill on the brakes discharge instead of charge?  

I'll be plugging them into other batteries tonight and I'm still thinking a video of me doing something really long and steep will happen.  I'll point the camera up a lot. I'm excited and totally confused and just capable of happiness with the low temps and what I think I'll be able to do.
```

## \#830 Posted by: Trillium Posted at: 2016-07-16T18:47:57.299Z Reads: 226

```
I'm not trying to derail this thread, but there was discussion above about a removable hub design.

I have a patent-pending universal design that uses hex nut, although the hex nut feature is not the main thing that sets it apart from other motors. I am releasing it soon under the Trillium brand (ridetrillium.com) and will need some help Beta testing if anyone is interested. It actually isn't really aimed at the DIY market (or at least the endless-sphere / eSk8 type crowd) but I think I will make it available to you guys. More on this soon.... I will make a thread at that point,

-Ev
```

## \#846 Posted by: whitepony Posted at: 2016-07-17T15:27:23.656Z Reads: 226

```
gave it a topspeed run over a distance of 400m straight, slight wind, measured avg speed back and forth in an upright normal riding stance, i.e. I brought the board up to max speed and pressed "record" in both directions once. before the runs I measured the voltage: 40.2V!

avg speed "->": 43.4kph
avg speed "<-": 43.4kph

so pretty consistent, also with all other runs I ever did with my vanguard! also, so far I never felt like there was anything holding this motor back - it always goes straight to top speed and stays there. difference of wind in my back vs. wind in my face for example: nada!

now, off to crunch numbers:
ESC: VESC in **FOC** mode, battery limit 50A, motor limit 70A
Motor: SK3 6374 168KV
Gearing: 16/36T with 15mm belt
Wheels: Flywheels 90mm 75A
Voltage: 40.2V

**Theoretical max speed: 50.9kph @ 6753 motor rpm**
**Average speed: 43.4kph @ 5758 motor rpm**

**= efficiency of 85.2%**

and thats in foc mode that is known to have slightly less top speed, so it compares with your 65% first run. 

I now really wonder where this pretty big gap comes from? in theory my board should be a lot worse: extremely soft big urethane chunks vs. hard slim urethane and a gates gt3 15mm belt vs. nothing! :confused:


btw, I carved home after that test and measured the urethane temperature when I stopped at home with a laser temperature gun: 47°C, ambient temperature 23°C.
```

---
## \#847 Posted by: Hummie Posted at: 2016-07-17T15:52:59.284Z Reads: 218

```
So I'm at 72% of the noload and ur at 85% to a similar speed. 
Actually ur big wheels probably have better rolling resistance.  Soft is a good thing as long as it has lots of rebound, especially on a less than smooth surface.  
Other than that your motor is much bigger with a stator of 5374 maybe and mine add up to 4750.  And then the lack of gearing beyond that.  If someone did a test with a similar sized stator without gearing it would be more revealing of the difference between pulley and hub.  But your test does show these little motors are being pushed beyond what they're best at for sure. 
Sadly I've broken 2 escs in 2 days.  One was my old Chaka one on FOC and one of the new ones I just put on from elkick lasted less than 2 charges and that was on bldc!  I think they're "mildly" broken as the Chaka one doesn't show a failure code. 
Didn't have time to do a temp test.  Of course the speed test here didn't kick it up anything other than warm to the touch.   from what I could tell from the short period I was riding the temp takes longer to rise likey partially because of the steel lack decreased thermal conductivity and also maybe the airgap is helping. Have to test more when I can but for shorter hard runs the bell stayed way cooler
```

## \#849 Posted by: whitepony Posted at: 2016-07-17T16:29:11.156Z Reads: 212

```
[quote="Hummie, post:847, topic:1002"]
So I'm at 72% of the noload and ur at 85% to a similar speed.
[/quote]

you shouldnt compare apples with oranges (bldc vs. foc). to make it comparable, its hub foc 65% vs. belt foc 85% and its pretty much the same topspeed too. if i went BLDC, my top speed would rise for the same voltage and then its probably 92% vs. 72% or so - either way the gap of roughly 20% would probably stay.

[quote="Hummie, post:847, topic:1002"]
Other than that your motor is much bigger with a stator of 5374 maybe and mine add up to 4750.  And then the lack of gearing beyond that.
[/quote]

but you have 2 hubs and I just have a single motor and your gearing is basically 90KV vs. 168KV? :confused:

[quote="devin, post:848, topic:1002"]
just curious how you measured the # of seconds from start to finish line? carrying video camera?
[/quote]

got a cycle gps app - when I press start it records distance and speed and when I press stop it tells me max and average. that app was a bit weird though, because the velocity kept "fading in" when I looked at the data. obviously this was bullshit since I hit start when I was at a stationary speed and during the whole run the motor frequency didnt change one bit. my guess is that its some smoothing kalman filter working behind the scenes of that app. I simply checked the speed graph and extracted the last stationary speed, which corresponded pretty much to the max speed (as expected, since my speed simply didnt change in those 400meters). perfectly flat pavement and motor frequency absolutely stable.
```

## \#856 Posted by: Hummie Posted at: 2016-07-17T17:27:46.262Z Reads: 196

```
The differences in kv I don't think are relevant as we are both at max throttle and hitting the same speed.  So as u say FOC to FOC and at the same speed.  So 65 to 85%. The differences between motor size are large, yes I'm in two but they add up to 47x50 stator and urs is much larger.   The best I can hope for is adding more copper and maybe do the femm program to possibly find the magnets aren't as big as they best...and next time the 4747 bit bigger stator.   The real test would be to get motors the same size and one with gearing and one with just a low kv maybe.  There are a lot of different tests possible but this would reveal how the increased load of a hubmotor would be more inefficient.
```

## \#857 Posted by: whitepony Posted at: 2016-07-17T17:57:39.702Z Reads: 226

```
flying start for me means you cross the start line already with stationary max speed? at least thats how I did it: hit max speed, hit record, hit stop before I had to brake down, gps trace done.

tried to export runtastic data for the last 30minutes, but you cant export from the app, only from the account and it looks like the data that is stored there is very sparse. more like a pointlist for a visual route drawing than high frequency gps trace data. got just these screenshots for now ... or a link to my runtastic events which show the velocity profile at least - not sure if you can access it: https://www.runtastic.com/de/benutzer/si-mi-43/sportaktivitaeten/578b9b70dcd33be81e8153ab (check the last stationary 200m - 43.44kph is actually the smallest value of the bunch of pretty much constant values. a few 0.x kph dont really matter in this anyway. on my way back the plateau is a lot shorter cause I had to overtake a bicycle. in the end only the obvious plateau matters though imo.).

<img src="/uploads/db1493/original/2X/1/1806aa30b57499171a13c4d24527a4912c845142.JPG" width="666" height="500">
<img src="/uploads/db1493/original/2X/8/8598a38889e07eb5b68feefa5ecdb8737a17516c.PNG" width="281" height="500">

[quote="Hummie, post:856, topic:1002"]
The differences in kv I don't think are relevant as we are both at max throttle and hitting the same speed.
[/quote]

its totally relevant, since you base your max theoretical speed calculation off the KV value and your current measured voltage. let say you think you got 90KV but in fact got 80KV motors - that means your max top speed is actually only 88.9% of what you think it is.
```

## \#861 Posted by: whitepony Posted at: 2016-07-17T18:16:06.360Z Reads: 210

```
we can agree on that - in fact to measure the efficiency you'd need the energy you put into the system and the energy you get out of it, i.e. the energy that works against air drag, tire deformation & co.

my experience though is: a motor will just always get to its max speed - low percentage inclines, low percentage downhill, wind in your back, wind in your face - all these things dont seem to make a difference - the motor somehow always reaches its desired rpm, which in my case on 2 boards is pretty much always 85% of the theoretical max speed. ive never experienced a slowing down of the motor because of a small incline or some extra wind thats helping or working against you. 

not sure how to explain it better - its a really weird feeling - like a car that has an electronic speed limit installed (like some german cars at 250kph) -> marching straight to that and then staying there.

the only time when a motor didnt reach this constant rpm was, when my gearing was too large for a 6355 motor, but Im still not sure if the vecs powered down from heat or the motor just didnt make it. once I changed the gearing a little, it also always went to its max speed, which also was about 85% of the max theoretical value.
```

---
## \#862 Posted by: whitepony Posted at: 2016-07-17T18:24:16.860Z Reads: 198

```
question remains though, why would the current hub motor only reach 65% of its max rpm in foc when all belt drives tend to get to 85% (not sure anymore in which thread quite a few people reported 85ish %)? do you think its under too much load, even with 2 hubs? is the heating up weakening the magnets or causing some significant extra resistance? do you know how much current you draw at top speed? maybe your battery is not strong enough and you have significant voltage sag?

could try 1 vs 2 vs 4 hubs to check if its a load issue
could try an ice cooled motor max speed test to rule heat out (like take it out of fridge with truck attached -> on board and gogogo :grinning: )
could try heavy parallel batteries to rule voltage sag out (you can also make voltage sag visible if you record vesc live data)
```

## \#864 Posted by: whitepony Posted at: 2016-07-17T18:32:54.215Z Reads: 202

```
[quote="devin, post:863, topic:1002"]
(more diameter = more torque)
[/quote]

torsional torque is "momentum x lever length" (lever = basically rotor radius), so its linearly increasing with diameter? wouldnt that mean that motor A is as strong as 2 motors B with half the diameter of motor A?

probably not that easy, since you can fit more iron and copper into a larger diameter too!
```

## \#866 Posted by: Hummie Posted at: 2016-07-17T18:45:21.384Z Reads: 217

```
I have two hub motors but they're small and definatey overtaxed with this challenge.  In my mind that's the reason I'm at such a low percent of the no-load.  To get the most efficiency out of such small motors, and still have them be hub motors, best bet would be a much lower kv and top speed.  But I want the speed!  It's a compromise for sure. I'll take small motors with the high kv with high speed burst ability over a big motor that can sustain the power endlessly.   In the future I can make this small size more powerful as there's still at least 2mm longer stator per motor and a lot more copper can be fit into it.   Next time.   All n all though I'm happy with these. The 80 duro rubber on the other hand dies too quickly.   
I could do the 90kv on 6s and see what percent of the no-load is.  With the decreased wind resistance at a max of around 15mph I bet id end up a better percent.
```

## \#884 Posted by: elkick Posted at: 2016-07-22T14:21:07.668Z Reads: 213

```
You're suffering from the calculation bug on "max current ramp step" in the advanced tab. Try setting it to 0.004 before applying "write config" and you're safe. The calculation is 10x higher than it should be, right default value is 0.04.
```

---
## \#885 Posted by: Justin Posted at: 2016-07-22T16:24:11.089Z Reads: 213


---
## \#886 Posted by: elkick Posted at: 2016-07-22T21:41:59.643Z Reads: 214

```
I am abroad currently not able to insert the fix right now. I think Jacob Bloy is also updating it on vesc.net.au. But to be honest, it might take a few days, that task isn't that easy as it seems. There is a bug free standalone version on github (which I am using when programming the VESCs we are selling). But in the BLDC tool itself still contains the old one in the corresponding directory on github.

To make it short: if you're programming your VESC with ST-Link under Ubuntu you'd be able to use the new version, if going through (ported) BLDC Tool versions on OSX or Win it might take some more time for a fixed version. Until then, please stick with the above suggestion of entering the values 10x lower (= 0.00400) in that field **every time before pressing "write config"**.
```

## \#897 Posted by: Hummie Posted at: 2016-07-23T21:21:45.192Z Reads: 236

```


now that I've sucked some of you in to get these motors the test is on to see what happens.  You  likely do know these are "experimental"!  Feedback is key.  The forum makes that easy or email or even call.  I think I've very likely ridden these more than all of you and things are looking good
 ....except:

I've had two shorts in the leads, one due to not enough shrink wrap and another from trying to contort the lead repeatedly and a magnet wire became disconnected from the main lead and now needs a soldering surgeon to get in there with a thin bit .  I tried to bend that motor lead too many directions around the hanger trying to figure where i wanted it to go.  Add more shrink wrap and dont try to contort their direction too much


things that have changed with the new steel motors:
they're all steel so no fear of damaging the shaft
a keyway to hold the stator (i fear for the aluminum version with my epoxy to hold them)
no bearing compression due to high heat expanding aluminum
a lot less heat transferred to the rotor's magnets and RUBBER.
not noticeably heavier.
look cool and are cooler than the aluminum but I have to do more testing to really see whats going on 

how are your motors doing?  In my mind as soon as the rubber gets sorted out I'm really happy with their performance.  I want the 80 again and am pouring it again but at higher heats it especially is trash quickly.  I am finding better stuff and I forget what I'm getting now but heat will no longer be an issue and 80 duro!  it's an actual rubber as apposed to polyurethane (which some people say is not really rubber) and it should have very similar qualities to skate polyurethane.  In my calling looking for tech guys at the biggest rubber manufacturers I chanced on one who is a skater and in my explaining the problem while on the phone with him looking at the steelhubs.com website he got excited.    He rounded up the other rubber gurus and we did a conference call.  they liked the challenge and rose to it and can whip something up.  I cant even pronounce it or remember it's accronym 

I'm using just red loctite with the steel no problem so far.
```

## \#900 Posted by: Hummie Posted at: 2016-07-24T21:33:57.783Z Reads: 243

```
mobutusan and justin am glad you guys got these and are "on the team"  .  That sounds goofy but I've told so many people that if they get the aluminum and later want to get the steel for 225 I feel we're on the same team.   The point of the team...is so we can we get to the 80 motor minimum order to get the steel made.   that's about 7000$.  75$ each is the cost they've told me and I think it will stay.   The steel are much easier for me to get ready as they have a keyway for the stator so things can be cheaper and a lot less time for me.   And red loctite is good with steel on both sides and that's easy.  Point is things are getting easier, ..better,.. moving forward.. the motivation to get the next thing made keeps me coming back to the lowest prices I can do as i have none saved and trying to build a "team" to get the 80 just like we did with the aluminum before.  

 I'm riding the steel all the time and they're great but we're learning through doing and I always want the next better thing and it will be more powerful and a bit lighter.  same size.
 2mm longer stator, stronger magnets (N48h instead of N45sh..never want to get to 300f anyway! https://www.kjmagnetics.com/specs.asp) that are a bit bigger.
How much stronger will these be and consequently how much cooler..we will see hopefully!!! not to diminish how great the steel are now and I should shut my mouth before things are sold.  I have total faith in the mechanical aspect of these (other than the rubber) and am just trying to get more power out of them now.

bigger stator and stronger magnets is magnetic strength so can have a low kv without relying on using many turns of necessarily thin wire.  Can have thicker wire, which will have less resistance, and therefore less heat, for the same torque.  

the rubber is no good and going to Epdm rubber.  polyurethane will always have problems especially if it's soft duro stuff.  mark my words all those companies selling hub motors..polyurethane deteriorates at heat and all hub motors have heat....unless they're huge and then they arent hub motors but direct drive.  you can add a core, but typically it's a hard polyurethane and that's the same problem.  There's maybe a core material that could work but I'm not going to go on a long goose chase and just go with different rubber.    companies on kickstarter selling polyurethane hub motors...I bet they have amp limits set so low that the performance sucks and yea then maybe you can use polyurethane.  I have yet to see a decent test of any hub motor's rubber.  there's no escaping polyurethanes max temps regardless if its hot or cold pour.  I remember seeing a pic of what was maybe the stary board or onboard..some hub motor company, and it had a max temp printed on the outside of the motor, wish I remember exactly what temp it said but think it said 160 or 180f.  Idont think I would expect the polyurethane to hold up for long staying under either of those limits
```

---

---
## \#902 Posted by: Hummie Posted at: 2016-07-25T03:46:29.732Z Reads: 231

```
trucks get warm.  that's ok.  get an infrared thermometer and keep under 200 really.  the motors are good to higher temps with no damage but the rubber deteriorates. keep an eye on it and I'll see if I can get this better stuff soon.

the glue is high temp epoxy and good for way higher.  I have had it give out on me once when I first did the steel motors because they had lots of cutting oil in them I didn't get out.  When the glue gives it's a slow movement and youre motor wires will get spun slowly around till they unplug.  doesnt spin quickly.  I'm just using red loctite and it hasn't given out.
```

## \#907 Posted by: Pylonflyer Posted at: 2016-07-25T20:24:20.643Z Reads: 229

```
Hummie, keep up the good work!  It will be interesting to see what type of tooling is required for processing the EPDM rubber.  It is indeed high temp stuff and is an interesting option.  You know the fascinating thing about your project journey is when you are using materials and techniques for one application (human powered skateboards) and then adapt them for a new one, it's at that point you find the limitations of what has always just been.  The heat generated from the motors is one of those variables that shows how assumptions in product development are dangerous.  In reality, you are very far along the process and imagine if you had paid thousands in tooling to some skate-wheel company to find the heat generated from hub-motors is too much for standard methods.  I'll be out riding my board some more in the next coming days to check heat, climb performance, and wheel wear.  I'm running 10S and am using the faster cure urethane, FYI.
```

## \#922 Posted by: Hummie Posted at: 2016-08-04T17:51:44.986Z Reads: 248

```
Ifmotor is made waterproof with epoxy on the steel stator and if also the connections between the windings and the motor leads are fully sealed the motor can run in the water no problem.  Can run under water.  Bearings don't like it though.

Getting better rubber made folks.  If ur riding around pushing ur motors hard the heat isn't good for polyurethane and they will lose their tight fit long before they wear down.  I'm getting the mold done for Epdm rubber and others to try so hold on and stay cool
```

---
## \#923 Posted by: laurnts Posted at: 2016-08-04T20:25:35.643Z Reads: 235

```
I know that brushless motor are waterproof by nature as there are no electronics in the inside except if you implement the hall sensor. However dust and particles can get in and ruin everything after a long run. These all adds up together and occasionally need to be cleaned up. It's not a big problem if the motor is detachable for regular maintenance, but if it's locked up with a loctite, then its hard to do maintenance.

Btw is the hub motor you provide can be delivered with a kegel hub wheels? I'd be even more interested if it is.
```

## \#924 Posted by: Hummie Posted at: 2016-08-04T20:33:12.752Z Reads: 251

```
The cost of sending stuff to u from here is a lot.  Maybe it's cheaper for me to send u the kegels as well but I doubt it. 
I've been riding for a couple hundred miles on a new set of motors, steel, and just opened them up 3 days ago.  Not a scratch to the magnets.  The motors have filtering screens on on side and adding them in the other side is possible too.  The bearings will eventually need replacing but that's doable.  (The smaller one easily the larger more work involved)
With the steel motors being steel I don't feel they NEED to have the bearings fixed with retaining fluid and i could send them without so the motor wouldn't need to be pressed apart for maintainence.  Up to u.  they will rattle a bit similar but less than a regular skate wheel without my fixing-down the bearings.  It's really minor but I like the silence.  U could get ur own retaining fluid
```

## \#935 Posted by: Mobutusan Posted at: 2016-08-09T16:43:45.984Z Reads: 276

```
Just thought I'd post this hack in case anyone out there wants to experiment with a quick, cheap, cooling duct idea for the aluminum hubs. It's made out of EPDM garage door sealer and zip ties  and is flexible, but stiff enough to hold its shape at some speed. Unfortunately, I don't have the time to do comparative testing to see if this has a noticeable effect, so if anyone else wants to test it, be my guest. FWIW, I can feel a decent amount of air come out of the sides of the hubs by just blowing air into the "duct" area.

<img src="/uploads/db1493/original/2X/2/2ccadea2984c9e35e753e35a72dca0124410df20.jpg" width="690" height="388">
<img src="/uploads/db1493/original/2X/5/5ebd46e2fda174ae493ae6987ab309ea5cacb847.jpg" width="690" height="388">
<img src="/uploads/db1493/original/2X/3/32fac205a0b8a2b8eacf0c375f927a7a0a325cd8.jpg" width="690" height="388">
```

## \#944 Posted by: Hummie Posted at: 2016-08-31T04:17:17.477Z Reads: 258

```
The motor will come to the ends of the rubber the same as it does now.  The motor will be just as recessed in the wheel and the motor will still just bolt on the end of a regular skate truck. Bit worried about the extra leverage with the long motor and looked for 10mm trucks but they generally stopped making them as supposedly the 8mm these days are much stronger.   
The huge contact patch.  In my experience which seems the same as caustins ..they are really good at rolling over stuff. 110mm is insane. Maybe it will cone more. Probably. So what. 

I never noticed much if any difference with one powered wheel vs two.  Two of these motors would be like 6 of what I ride now.
```

## \#957 Posted by: Wanderer Posted at: 2016-09-01T03:30:37.510Z Reads: 254

```
For a single set up it would be ideal to shave down one side of the hanger and make it symmetrical, when something sticks out that far from normal you just know your going to catch it on something....and it looks goofy. From what I have read it is not hard on most trucks to do. For someone doing a dual setup it would be easier to just get a narrower truck and save the mods.

What about just keeping the design you have the same but making the inner threads go way deeper? This way if you wanted to use it as a single you could modify one side of the truck hanger and make some more threads on the axle, and if someone wanted to do a dual set up they could just get a narrower hanger and use the very same motor design, their axle would just not use all the extra internal threads.
```

## \#959 Posted by: Wanderer Posted at: 2016-09-01T03:57:43.607Z Reads: 260

```
Yea, maybe easier to just have specific motors for dual or single.

Someone who has actually done it may be able to answer you better on how easy it is. From what I remember you can press the axle out, cut the hanger and than press the axle back in, but this requires a press of some sort, or you can put it in a lathe and tool the aluminum down. What I would do without a machine shop at my disposal is hack saw a nice straight cut through the cast aluminum all the way around the inner axle being carful not to cut the axle, than take a pair of vicegrips or put in a vice and twist the excess off, apply heat if necassary. In my head it would work....
```

## \#974 Posted by: Hummie Posted at: 2016-09-29T02:34:44.718Z Reads: 280

```
  90mm on those small motors would be too taxing.  Whenu kill those motors since u helped me from the beginning I'll give u one or two of the biggie motors cheap as I can.  Ultimately all those aluminum with their small only shielded  bearings and the high heat epoxy and the retaining fluid will all be a pain down the road. People are going to want to replace bearings and they won't be able to do it themselves without a couple hundred in tools. The high heat epoxy is crazy strong and it needs to be cracked to get to the big bearing which are only shielded.  The new big motors are 1144 steel and are strong enough to be a slide fit for the bearings and people can replace parts themselves, and the all steel allows just loctite to hold the motors on and that's much easier to unscrew and get to the big bearing when you need to.   Sealed much big bearings. Motors u can go anywhere and not worry about heat. Rubber that doesn't deteriorate with heat.  Steps toward an indestructible monster that's easily serviceable
```

## \#988 Posted by: Hummie Posted at: 2016-10-13T17:07:11.645Z Reads: 264

```
the nitrile is super sticky and good...except what is stated as 80 duro seemed much harder for about ten minutes in the hands and has now settled at maybe 75 and then when it gets hot it drops all the way down to maybe 70 and I rolled a tire twice when I heated it up a lot.   the good news is I ordered us all 85 and 90 duro in this stuff and 80 and 85 duro hydrogenated (high heat), for both motors.   But Im more excited that I've tracked down some really good high heat, hot pour polyurethane I'm getting samples of and will pour that too and it's on the way.  This stuff is top notch and although the nitrile is good I know it doesnt have the rebound of polyurethane and that equates to greater range on a charge and more durability and syched to be pouring that stuff.  

 @evoheyax beats motors up more than anyone I've encountered with his weight and extremely hilly long commute and after replacing all the bearings with full ceramics after he cooked the lube out of the steel, we found it's definitely nice to have it all slide fittings so we can get the bearings off and on without exotic tools and that's what the new motors will be.   With (2) two-pin spanner wrenches found on amazon we'll be able to access everything to replace bearings or windings or whatever.


the big motos should be here in about a week!!.   they had trouble making magnets that are 70x3mm and they kept breaking
```

---
## \#989 Posted by: Mobutusan Posted at: 2016-10-13T19:39:20.827Z Reads: 258

```
@hummie Do you have a link for a cheap spanner wrench that you like on Amazon? I'm not sure which one to get, but I need to open up one of my motors. The red Loctite I used on the axle threads let loose while I was riding and the wires got twisted and pulled out a little bit. One seems to still work fine and one just stutters now.
```

---
## \#990 Posted by: Hummie Posted at: 2016-10-13T19:50:53.662Z Reads: 259

```
sorry to hear.  especially sorry to hear because I'd rather use red loctite.  Had you covered the threads and cranked it down?  I use red now with no problem so far.  Rather not use the crazy strong two-part high-heat epoxy because its too strong.

 it's very likely fixable.  Worse is if one of the wires is shorting to another through the center piece but that's likley not whats happened and you just need to make a better connection again.  

To get the older motors apart you need to press them apart.  you also need the wrench.  I dont have the details on what size pins you need on that wrench now as I'm at work,  dont have a measuring tape handy, but you'll need an adjustable two-pin spanner or wrench and it has to have pins that are maybe 3mm max diameter.   Measure the size of the holes on the threaded ring on the inside and thats what youll need to get into, but like I say those motors are also glued shut after you get that ring off and you'll need a press.  I have one and you can bring it by and I'll do it all.   you'll likely need to resolder the lead to the winding though with a fine tip solder iron which I dont have.
```

---
## \#991 Posted by: Mobutusan Posted at: 2016-10-13T22:03:57.111Z Reads: 259

```
Thanks for the info. Ok try to get them apart, but I may end up having to bring them down to you. I just have no idea when. And I'm not sure why the loctite came undone, but the hubs were moneyed onto a Randall II truck, and maybe they didn't have long enough axles or enough thread engagement or something. It sure looked like there was a lot of loctite still on the threads and axles, but I'll try more next time.
```

---
## \#992 Posted by: Hummie Posted at: 2016-10-13T22:37:53.813Z Reads: 267

```
Probably because they're aluminum and steel holds better.  

Ive just been loctiting the steel..  The other glue I used to use I think we estimated at 200 foot pounds of torque to get them off. A motor I forget but maybe will do teens. 

The wires though are thin and aluminum. Crappy. Read that fat wires a good heatsink. Next time they're supposed to be 12gauge copper. I'm liking a fat wire coming out and turning into a heatsink!  U can get the same electrical resistance I thin in a copper sheet as a wire. If that's true why not have thin sheeting at least.  Thermally conductive insulator there. That's doable.
```

---
## \#1005 Posted by: Hummie Posted at: 2016-10-24T21:15:58.746Z Reads: 265

```
i only have the aluminum now.  i have to get a couple more of the regular smaller steel motors in the future because they already made them.   the improvements going forward forgetting those few I'll have to get are bigger motors obviously.  almost 3x as big or long.  both bearings are now big.  and sealed not just shielded.  and there's two types of steel in the future, 1144 for the center shaft and 1018 for the rotor.  what the bearings are sitting on is much stronger than a standard skate axle.   and bigger copper multistrand silicone wires.  it was going to be 100% magnet fill but they couldnt do it, it's hard to get 70mmx3mm magnets in there, so they're working on doing it the 80% fill as before.  that's been the hold-up...trying to get magnets in.  it'll happen.

I'm going to leave it all tight slide fittings so you if you get (2) two-pin wrenches for maybe 20$ each you can open everything up and replace anything.
```

---

## \#1014 Posted by: Pylonflyer Posted at: 2016-11-01T18:01:53.967Z Reads: 289

```
**I'll testify that the Aluminum Hub motors are great for all around boarding.  I have one of the original sets (dual motors) from Hummie and I ride it daily around the neighborhood to exercise my dog.  I'm running 10S and it has the power to go up decent grade hills and have never had a heat issue.  I usually cruise at about 15mph with top speed at around 23mph when my dog is spazzing out when we first go.  Love the power, the braking, and the silent/stealth quietness over my old belt driven board.  I'm running 2 VESCs from Enertion FOC at 60 amp motor peak.  Test run video showing temps around various terrain.  Oh, and I have original urethane tires of the "fast cure" urethane with no sign of wear, fyi.  I have since built proper enclosures.**
https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=pXNHvNgz5kw
```

## \#1021 Posted by: Hummie Posted at: 2016-11-16T18:20:27.005Z Reads: 266

```
?  

i have two more of the first 8 prototype long motors available as someone backed out.  

300$ for the motor mounted and a pair of Paris V2 and an extra tire.

it took a shockingly long time to finish the details for these but finally they are being made now.  the good news is the center threaded hole in the motor will have 25mm of threading, so I'll put the hangers on the lathe and further thread the axle and the motors will recessed further along the axle.  the motors will sit 18mm further inside than the rest of the wheels.  so sticking out 18mm to the inside and 101-56-18=..so.27mm to the outside.    more centered and more surface for the motor to be adhered to the axle

12 gauge copper silicone motor leads sticking upward toward the board only.  screens on both sides of the motor.
```

## \#1034 Posted by: Hummie Posted at: 2016-12-29T17:18:42.005Z Reads: 247

```
The new motors will be in my hands in likely 3 days!   Stuck on vacation in la when I could be back riding.  

Anyway I'll definitely figure the max continuous wattage on these and will likely be 150% roughly of what the small pair we're (which was never tested).

By the profile I think you mean width. With both motors on one truck I'm not sure.  The design allows me to mount them far along the axle but with two that would be a lot of hp and little hanger left if I put them far along trying to get narrow.  If you want to see what's possible I can do it squeezed far along and you can tell me how it goes. I don't know

Resplacing a hub with a wheel...possible but work removing the motor and then you'd likely have to add spacers (speed rings maybe) to where the hanger aluminum had been cut back as I just wrote about above. Or don't have it cut back and go extra wide and replace easier
```

## \#1046 Posted by: Hummie Posted at: 2016-12-31T21:19:08.602Z Reads: 258

```
with hub motor's simplicity and these attaching to any threaded 5/16 shaft I think these open up possibilities!  The power to transport a human or more is there at a fraction of the weight and cost of a rediculous combustion vehicle.   how to best utilize the power of two or four hub motors? You could stand on it but you'd be missing out.  I'd rather be in a bucket seat inches from the ground. I fantasize about what quickness and speeds could happen with 4 or 6 on something maybe as simple as a super long deck laid on and some handles to hold on. Hopefully can calculate based on performance of 2.  Street luging at rocket speed wouldn't mix with cars though.  Something else. More control.  More upright. I'm sure it could be made a million ways but I don't have one
```

## \#1092 Posted by: devin Posted at: 2017-01-09T16:57:12.088Z Reads: 224

```
The dual setup is the way to go in my opinion with trucks shaft shaved down. 



NICE WORK.
```

## \#1142 Posted by: Hummie Posted at: 2017-01-14T19:06:32.285Z Reads: 236

```
when I was a kid I wanted those gas motors and rc planes but never had the bucks and later more so wanted to make a 3/4 scale World War one bi or tri wing replica you can fly.    Make it in your garage and take off from the local soccer field to cuba.  Only 80 yard take off and landing room needed.   More appealing to me than making a human lifting drone.   OO the control-line plane...on a string...doing circles...I get you now.

a huge improvement was finally listening to devin and cranking my motor amps up to 200 on the vesc.  I'm really ecstatic with the new motor and the vesc at 200 motor limit.  smoother, quieter, less cogging, even throttle, and full power from lowest speeds.  I recommend trying it...although Ive only ridden maybe 2 hours without anything blowing up I think it'll be fine.
```

## \#1145 Posted by: Hummie Posted at: 2017-01-14T22:56:23.792Z Reads: 232

```
yes as you say and the ratio is very important for the smooth throttle at least and there's the math somewhere to figure the ratio but luckily for me it was done for me.  if you just crank up to 200 motor amps and dont do the ratio at least at slow speeds you will have more power regardless of the battery number.  a high battery number giving you more power at higher speeds.  the low speed punch makes the slower speed riding much more fun and I'm not afraid of traffic as much with it.  
I wonder if it's maybe more inefficient, although I dont really care and will gladly take the better performance, but with more amps being pumped through at less than 100percent duty cycle.  (100percent duty cycle being on full time) . putting it up to 200motor amps is increasing the power that is being used at less than 100percent duty cycle when the esc is less efficient (and I heard also the motor) but these inefficiencies are pretty small.

  between riding and fixing broken things and this and that laziness I dont get much testing done.  More so riding comes first and then fixing in that order and it's always the receiver or the canbus.  but found my iphone and my logging chip and will be at it. things move slow.

I have some sensors I was planning to add and try but now more than ever I dont feel its worth it.  another part to break and complexity when its pretty damn good already.
```

## \#1214 Posted by: wmj259 Posted at: 2017-01-23T04:22:22.005Z Reads: 207

```
There's five types of failure theories in Mechanics of Materials class,  one is maximum shear stress,  maximum distortion energy,  von misses stress,  normal stress and mohrs  circle.. Materials have different properties that allow one material to be more brittle or ductile or just stronger then the other. 
Depending on what the material is results in the type of crack or destruction it has. For example a rod is used for tranferring torque from one location to another. The way it would break if it was be used for torque transfer would be like this:
<img src="/uploads/db1493/original/3X/5/1/51cba2281dd3a6b58b09578ac8dbe6ce36febfcf.jpg" width="534" height="500">
Shear fracture. Just like cutting a piece of paper with scissors. Would look like this.
<img src="/uploads/db1493/original/3X/e/e/ee137bb2009d387e841f11e39d7ca88f390e51f3.jpg" width="349" height="310">

Another would be used for materials being stretched as in cable wires for bridges or beams. 
They would fracture from necking or buckling.
<img src="/uploads/db1493/original/3X/c/7/c7f77546f204f0b67bcbb95e98b901a8456ae438.png" width="620" height="186">

Since this wasn't a case of transferring torque from the hub to the axle, IMHO it would be shear stress.
Can you show a picture of what the axle/truck side looks like? So far we only see a "bolt"? inside the hub motor unless thats the axle.
```

## \#1226 Posted by: Wanderer Posted at: 2017-01-23T07:08:48.992Z Reads: 201

```
Would have to agree with devin here, it seems to me to be quite obvious that this is the result of using a dremel tool on the axle.
Experiment, put a solid steel bar in a vice, put a small groove around the shaft with a dremel disc, and than put a snipe on the steel bar and apply force across the length of the bar...guess where it will break. Especially if it is high carbon steel. Ever see high carbon metal sheets brake in half like a cracker with a line scored into it? This is what devin was referencing about glass....I think. 
Basically what this long motor is doing is acting like the snipe on the end of the solid steel bar, compound this with the snipe(motor) being butted up against an immovable object(cast part of axle), and the snipe not being able to move either, now you have a a pulling force being applied to the weak spot when ever the snipe(motor) moves up. Repeat this action thousands of times in small amounts with every rock, crack, uneven surface, etc that this motor would have seen, and you get the result we are all looking at.
Apply twisting force and it simply adds another source of fatigue.
```

## \#1229 Posted by: HTownBomber Posted at: 2017-01-23T16:31:25.406Z Reads: 199

```
I agree with this analysis, particularly the effect of the hanger butted right up to the steel stator tube. Much of steel's strength lies in its elastic modulus; the ability of steel to bend elastically and spring back into place in response to stress. Your Rojas Hybrids do this. In this case, the axle had no room to move and the combined shear/torque forces were translated to a single location along the axial plane. Shear and torque forces were introduced, there was no "give" along the steel axle and the stator tube ultimately sheared the axle at its weakest point.

To visualize this, think about a metal screw; you can bend it with a hammer / pliers but typically you can't break it without cutting tools. The exception is when you drive it completely into a piece of metal or wood and keep on torquing (or hit it with a hammer for that matter.) It'll eventually shear at the only spot it can still deform, right at the head.
```

## \#1266 Posted by: Hummie Posted at: 2017-01-23T21:42:30.377Z Reads: 184

```
if you go to endless sphere and search devin you'll find that the current is smoothed out by inductors and you don't have moments of huge torque during low duty cycle with the pwm.  i've told you this many times as you've told me this many times.   I'll cut and paste for you here two people who design and sell electric motors confirming this on es.

 "With the 10% duty cycle, you won't have 100 amps for ON portion or the cycle and 0 amps for the OFF portion. Instead you'll see something like 8 amps ramping up to 12 amps during the ON portion,and that 12 amps gradually ramps down to 8 amps during the OFF portion. On average it stays pretty close to 10 amps, and will have basically the same heat generation as if it was a steady 10 amps at 100% duty. "

"the motor winding inductance filters the current into a fairly smooth value. The huge current pulses are from the capacitor bank through the FETs."
```

## \#1290 Posted by: Hummie Posted at: 2017-01-23T22:37:34.230Z Reads: 210

```
"It is a common misconception that stainless steel is stronger than regular steel. In fact, due to their low carbon content, many stainless steel alloys cannot be hardened through heat treatment. Therefore, when compared to regular steel, the stainless alloys used in bolts are slightly stronger than an un-hardened (grade 2) steel but significantly weaker than hardened steel "
```

## \#1294 Posted by: 3sly Posted at: 2017-01-23T23:07:38.813Z Reads: 229

```
I didn't want to get myself involved in the conversation. But Devin, even though I'm sure you mean it well, your way of arguing and pushing your unscientific opinion is really tiresome and a bit frustrating. Not to mention your often faulty arguments used

As a mechanical engineer with a plethora of courses in material structural sciences I suspect:

* This is as far as I can tell in no way whatsoever a torsional failure. Torsional failures do often not occur in one plane (as happened here). The dremel incisions could however have induced a torsional failure in one plane (together with the clamping behind the incisions of the axle) but then the failure would induce microvoids in the center as depicted in figure 1, and not fault lines like here...
* The parallel fault lines indicate a bending fracture. This could indeed be because of the fact that the longer motor induces an adequate extra leverage on the axle. But the main reason is FATIGUE. Steel itself is plenty strong to cope with the slightly larger leverage induced by the setup. Fatigue however made the crack propagate until the point of uncontrolled propagation. The incisions of the dremel were already deeper than the max stable depth that a crack can propagate, beyond that depth a crack propagates uncontrollably and fails eg. BREAKS
* Devin is however right about the fact that the dremel incisions have played a large role in the fracture itself. In any fatigue induced fracture: giving the axle a place to initiate the fatigue (as small as the initiated cut may seem), plays a big role in the decrease of the fatigue resistance. Not only this cut, but the fact that the clamping face of the axle is right behind the cut, explains why the fracture occured in a smooth plane almost flush with the rest of the truck. Not to mention the continuous and large varying bending forces that are present.

bending + leverage + fatigue + crack initiation = failure

Can I say that the failure would not have occured if the cracks weren't present? I don't know. But I do know it's a margin too thin to count on, by that I mean: there should be a structural improvement made to make sure this doesn't happen again.

But in no way whatsoever was this a failure due to torsion.

Oh: and let's not rule out you might have a bad batch of trucks. Don't always count to religiously on the quality of manufactured items.

Can we now move on to thinking of ways to fix the problem and the more fun things.

Again: Devin, don't be mad at me :p I mean it well, but I just had to correct you there.

Dries

Picture 1
[http://classes.mst.edu/civeng120/lessons/torsion/fracture/a36_torsion_fracture_2.jpg](http://classes.mst.edu/civeng120/lessons/torsion/fracture/a36_torsion_fracture_2.jpg)
```

## \#1306 Posted by: PB1 Posted at: 2017-01-24T08:28:21.481Z Reads: 198

```
@devin, your hand-drawn picture in post 1227 is certainly flawed. 
A longer motor definitely creates a larger lever and more force on the axle. 

@hummie, don't give up!!!!

Your large motors looks great and would be a good alternative to what is around elsewhere. 

Btw I also think that dremeling was one of the main issues here, but for other reasons than devin mentioned. 
Your dremeling probably created a surface on the hanger that was not 100% perpendicular to the axle.  
Then the motor with the large lever was allowed to wiggle up and down while riding which probably caused the axle to break due to fatigue. 

So the ideal hanger for your motor 
a) would be very thick in diameter in order to have a large contact area with your motor
b) would be machined to have a nice and perfectly plane area
c) in order for the hanger to be machined, the axle needs to be removable

no idea if such a hanger exists.
```

```
Why make it complicated and calculate wattage?

@hummie had motors, they were attached to hangers and the axles didn't brake.

=> larger and very plane contact area where hanger meets motor
```

## \#1322 Posted by: Maxid Posted at: 2017-01-24T13:10:05.002Z Reads: 193

```
[quote="PB1, post:1317, topic:1002, full:true"]
I haven't heard of a broken axle (I have dremeled hangers and haven't broken an axle). 
[/quote]
I think that is why he thinks torque must be the problem here. usually you have belt drive systems where the wheel sits on bearings and the axle only has to withstand gravity. With a hub motor the stator core actually needs to stay in place. So the axle does need to withstand that additional torque exerted by the motor.
Personally however I doubt that this indeed has anything to do with torque and believe this is just like @3sly and you said.
```

## \#1340 Posted by: mmaner Posted at: 2017-01-24T20:46:26.557Z Reads: 279

```
[quote="Hummie, post:1339, topic:1002"]
@mmaner wrote to me that maybe there should be a gap between the motor and mount
[/quote]

I'm just guessing, as I haven't seen the manner of construction.  It makes sense that spreading the stress across a longer surface should lessen the possibility of stress cracks/fractures in the substrate.
```

## \#1360 Posted by: evoheyax Posted at: 2017-01-25T16:50:57.677Z Reads: 377

```
The solution to me is to optimize the smaller motors. I was just reading a post that mentioned motors below 100 kv, the copper is too thin, and causes a lot of heat buildup and losses.

He might be able to fit a bigger stator in there also, and the bearing choice. There's ways to optimize the small motors. I'm still planning on a 4wd with the smaller motors complete. Just need to work out a deal with a VESC supplier.
```

---
## \#1361 Posted by: devin Posted at: 2017-01-25T16:52:34.141Z Reads: 383

```
<p>This post was flagged by the community and is temporarily hidden.</p>
```

---
## \#1362 Posted by: evoheyax Posted at: 2017-01-25T16:56:47.662Z Reads: 380

```
Too much power is not the problem though IMO. I think the real problem is that 16 AWG wire is being used in a high amp situation. 16 AWG wire is not meant for high current motors like what we need. I'm temped to rewind my 4wd motors to a high kv with a ticker gauge wire to see how it compares in heat. Or to get 4 new motors winded this way.
```
